Parsing [sent. 1577 len. 17]: The slight wounds made by these bamboos brought on lock-jaw , and too often terminated fatally .
Parsing [sent. 1578 len. 25]: In the attacks upon us at Rangoon they made their approaches with some degree of military skill , throwing up trenches as they advanced .
Parsing [sent. 1579 len. 11]: Their fire-rafts on such a rapid river were also formidable .
Parsing [sent. 1580 len. 33]: They have wells of petroleum up the country : their rafts were very large , and on them , here and there , were placed old canoes filled with this inflammable matter .
Parsing [sent. 1581 len. 28]: When on fire , it blazed as high as our maintop , throwing out flames , heat , and stink quite enough to drive any one away .
Parsing [sent. 1582 len. 45]: I have mentioned their mode of warfare and their deficiencies , to prove that if the Burmahs had been as well provided with every species of arms equal to our own , the country would not have been so soon subjugated as it was .
Parsing [sent. 1583 len. 19]: Their system of defence was good , their bravery was undoubted , but they had no effective weapons .
Parsing [sent. 1584 len. 35]: I strongly suspect that they will , now that they have been taught their inferiority , use every means to obtain them ; and if so , they will really become a formidable nation .
Parsing [sent. 1585 len. 48]: As one proof of their courage , I will mention , that at every stockade there is a look-out man , perched on a sort of pole , about ten feet or more clear of the upper part of the stockade , in a situation completely exposed .
Parsing [sent. 1586 len. 50]: I have often observed these men , and it was not till the cannonade had fairly commenced on both sides , that they came down , and when they did , it was without hurry ; indeed , I may say , in a most leisurely and indifferent manner .
Parsing [sent. 1587 len. 11]: Of their invulnerables and their antics I have already spoken .
Parsing [sent. 1588 len. 17]: In countries governed despotically , life is not so much valued as it is in others .
Parsing [sent. 1589 len. 25]: The very knowledge that it may be taken in a moment at the will of the rulers , renders even the cowardly comparatively indifferent .
Parsing [sent. 1590 len. 24]: Having been accustomed from our earliest years to anticipate an event , when it actually arrives we meet it with composure and indifference .
Parsing [sent. 1591 len. 46]: The lad in England who is brought up to thieving , and who is continually reminded by his parents , that he _ must be hung _ before he is twenty goes to the gallows when his turn comes with much _ sang froid _ .
Parsing [sent. 1592 len. 32]: So it is in a despotic country , where the people witness the heads of their companions roll on the ground , and surmise how soon their own turn will come .
Parsing [sent. 1593 len. 12]: I had more than one evidence of this during my stay .
Parsing [sent. 1594 len. 17]: In one instance I wished to obtain information from a prisoner , but could extract none .
Parsing [sent. 1595 len. 43]: He had been sitting between the carronades on deck for twenty-four hours , and some of the men or officers had given him a bowl of grog and a couple of cigars , with which he was busy when I interrogated him .
Parsing [sent. 1596 len. 49]: As he professed ignorance , I told him that if he would not give me the desired information , I should take his head off ; and I sent for the serjeant of marines , who appeared with two of his party , and with his drawn sword .
Parsing [sent. 1597 len. 55]: We called him out from between the guns , but he begged through the interpreter to be allowed to finish his grog , to which I consented : when that was done , he was again ordered out , but requested leave to finish about an inch of cigar which remained in his mouth .
Parsing [sent. 1598 len. 22]: To which I also acceded , not being in a particular hurry to do that which I never intended to do .
Parsing [sent. 1599 len. 21]: During all this the man was perfectly composed , and did not show the least alarm at his approaching fate .
Parsing [sent. 1600 len. 20]: As soon as the cigar was finished , he bound his long hair up afresh , and made preparation .
Parsing [sent. 1601 len. 63]: I again asked him if he would tell , but he pleaded ignorance , and stepped forward , went down on his knees and took off the cloth from about his loins , which he spread on the deck to receive his head , and then putting his hands on the deck , held it in the position to be cut off .
Parsing [sent. 1602 len. 12]: Not a muscle trembled , for I watched the man carefully .
Parsing [sent. 1603 len. 38]: He was , of course , remanded , and the sailors were so pleased with him , that he went on shore with more grog and more tobacco than he had probably ever seen in his life .
Parsing [sent. 1604 len. 33]: The Burmahs have , however , a means of extracting information from spies , etcetera , which I never saw practised by them , although it was borrowed from them by us .
Parsing [sent. 1605 len. 30]: It was in our own quarter-master-general 's office that I witnessed this species of torture , so simple in its operation , and apparently so dreadful in its effects .
Parsing [sent. 1606 len. 25]: It consists in giving one single blow upon the region of the heart , so as to stop for some seconds the whole circulation .
Parsing [sent. 1607 len. 82]: The way by which this is effected is as follows : -- the man -- the Burmahs are generally naked to the waist -- is made to sit down on the floor ; another man stands behind him , and leaning over him , takes a very exact aim with his sharp bent elbow at the precise spot over his heart , and then strikes a blow which , from its being propelled so very mechanically , descends with increased force .
Parsing [sent. 1608 len. 34]: The effect appears dreadful ; the dark hue of the sufferer 's face turns to a deadly white ; the perspiration bursts out from his forehead , and he trembles in every limb .
Parsing [sent. 1609 len. 7]: I never witnessed such apparent agony .
Parsing [sent. 1610 len. 24]: These blows repeated three or four times , will unman the most resolute , and they will call for death as a favour .
Parsing [sent. 1611 len. 44]: There is one point which must not be overlooked by the Indian government , and which , connected with what I have already mentioned , makes the Burmese nation more formidable ; it is , the great contempt they have for the sepoys .
Parsing [sent. 1612 len. 15]: And what is equally true , the fears which the sepoys have of them .
Parsing [sent. 1613 len. 17]: The Burmahs are only afraid of the white faces , as I shall very soon establish .
Parsing [sent. 1614 len. 12]: They despise the sepoys , although they are so well armed .
Parsing [sent. 1615 len. 56]: Now , that the sepoys are good troops , there can be no doubt ; they have proved it often ; but , at the same time , they are not , as some of the Indian officers have asserted in my presence , the best troops in the world , and preferable to Europeans .
Parsing [sent. 1616 len. 51]: That they are much easier to control , and that they excel in discipline , I grant , because they are never intoxicated ; but they have , in the first place , very little stamina , and are , generally speaking , a small and very effeminately built race .
Parsing [sent. 1617 len. 37]: Still they have fought well -- very well ; but they never fought well against the Burmahs ; and for this simple reason , -- that superstition is more powerful than courage , and subdues it .
Parsing [sent. 1618 len. 44]: The sepoys are very superstitious , and had the idea , which was never eradicated , that the Burmahs were _ charmed men _ , and they never went out against them willingly , even when they were headed by the English troops .
Parsing [sent. 1619 len. 13]: As for the Burmahs ' contempt of them , it was notorious .
Parsing [sent. 1620 len. 64]: I have myself seen one of the Burmah prisoners at Rangoon lift up a piece of timber that six of the sepoys could hardly have moved , and throw it down , so as to make it roll at the feet of the sepoy guard who watched him , making them all retreat several paces , and then laugh at them in derision .
Parsing [sent. 1621 len. 8]: But we had many more decisive proofs .
Parsing [sent. 1622 len. 19]: The Burmahs had stockaded themselves about seven miles from Rangoon , and it was determined to dislodge them .
Parsing [sent. 1623 len. 33]: Colonel S -- , who was very partial to the native troops , was ordered on this service , and he requested particularly that he might have no troops but the sepoys .
Parsing [sent. 1624 len. 39]: Sir A Campbell did not much like to consent , but , as the stockades were not higher than breastworks , and the Burmahs not in very great force , he eventually yielded to the Colonel 's arguments .
Parsing [sent. 1625 len. 15]: Fifteen hundred sepoys were ordered out , and the Colonel went on his expedition .
Parsing [sent. 1626 len. 39]: The Burmahs had good intelligence that there were no European troops , and when the sepoys arrived , they did not wait to be attacked , but attacked the sepoys , and put them completely to the rout .
Parsing [sent. 1627 len. 32]: One half of the sepoys were said to be killed ; the others came back to Rangoon in parties of ten or twelve , and in the utmost consternation and confusion .
Parsing [sent. 1628 len. 24]: Sir A Campbell was , of course , much annoyed , and the next day a European force was despatched against the Burmahs .
Parsing [sent. 1629 len. 11]: On their arrival they witnessed a dreadful and disgusting scene .
Parsing [sent. 1630 len. 41]: A long avenue had been cut in the wood , and on each side of it were hung by the heels , at equal distances , shockingly mutilated , the naked bodies of the seven hundred and fifty sepoys killed .
Parsing [sent. 1631 len. 23]: The Burmahs did not , however , attempt to resist the European force , but after a few shots made their retreat .
Parsing [sent. 1632 len. 28]: Now , this is a very important fact : and it is a fact which can not be denied , although it has not been made known .
Parsing [sent. 1633 len. 87]: In India there is a nominal force of three hundred thousand men ; but they are scattered over such a vast extent of territory , that , allowing they could be made disposable , which they could not , it would require many months before they could be collected , and if the Burmahs despise the sepoys , and the sepoys dread the Burmahs , the only check against the latter will be the European troops ; and of them how many can be called out .
Parsing [sent. 1634 len. 26]: Not ten thousand , at the very utmost ; and the difficulty of collecting them was well known at the commencement of the Burmah war .
Parsing [sent. 1635 len. 62]: There certainly is a great difference between attacking others in their own territories , and defending ourselves ; but if the Burmahs could hold out against us , as they did , for nearly three years , without arms to cope with us , what might be the consequence if they were supplied with arms and officers by any other nation ?
Parsing [sent. 1636 len. 37]: We have now a footing in the country , and it must be our object to prevent the ingress of any other , and to keep the Burmahs as quiet and as peaceable as we can .
Parsing [sent. 1637 len. 37]: But our very intercourse will enlighten them by degrees , and we have more to dread from that quarter than from all the hordes of Russia or Runjeet Sing , and the whole disaffection of India .
Parsing [sent. 1638 len. 30]: As I have more to say relative to the Burmahs , I will , in my next chapter , enter into a short narrative of the expedition to Bassein .
Parsing [sent. 1639 len. 30]: It was a bloodless one , although very important in its results : and circumstances occurred in it which will throw much light upon the character of the nation .
Parsing [sent. 1640 len. 4]: CHAPTER TWENTY SIX .
Parsing [sent. 1641 len. 38]: It was not until many months after the war had been carried on , that Sir Archibald Campbell found himself in a position to penetrate into the heart of the Burmah territory , and attempt the capital .
Parsing [sent. 1642 len. 25]: He wanted almost every thing , and among the rest reinforcements of men ; for the rainy season had swept them off by thousands .
Parsing [sent. 1643 len. 55]: At last , when determined to make the attempt , he did it with a most inadequate force ; so small that , had the Burmahs thought of even trenching up and barricading the roads at every half mile , he must have been compelled , without firing a shot , to have retreated .
Parsing [sent. 1644 len. 22]: Fortunately , he had an accession of men-of-war , and his river detachment was stronger than he could have hoped for .
Parsing [sent. 1645 len. 50]: I do not pretend to state the total force which was embarked on the river or that which proceeded by hand , communicating with each other when circumstances permitted , as the major part of the provisions of the army were , I believe , carried up by water .
Parsing [sent. 1646 len. 54]: The united river force was commanded by Brigadier Cotton , Captain Alexander , and Captain Chads ; the land forces , of course , by Sir A Campbell , who had excellent officers with him , but whose tactics were of no use in this warfare of morass , mud , and jungle .
Parsing [sent. 1647 len. 21]: It will be proper to explain why it was considered necessary to detach a part of the forces to Bassein .
Parsing [sent. 1648 len. 23]: The Rangoon river joins the Irrawaddy on the left , about one hundred and seventy miles from its flowing into the ocean .
Parsing [sent. 1649 len. 51]: On the right of the Irrawaddy is the river of Bassein , the mouth of it about one hundred and fifty miles from that of the Irrawaddy , and running up the country in an angle towards it until it joins it about four hundred miles up in the interior .
Parsing [sent. 1650 len. 44]: The two rivers thus enclose a large delta of land , which is the most fertile and best peopled of the Burmah provinces , and it was from this delta that Bundoola , the Burmah general , received all his supplies of men .
Parsing [sent. 1651 len. 47]: Bundoola was in the strong fortress of Donabue , on the Bassein side of the river , about half way between where the Rangoon river joined it on the left , and the Bassein river communicated with it a long way farther up on the right .
Parsing [sent. 1652 len. 60]: Sir A Campbell 's land forces were on the left of the river , so that Bundoola 's communication with the Bassein territory was quite open ; and as the river forces had to attack Donabue on their way up , the force sent to Bassein , was to take him in the rear and cut off his supplies .
Parsing [sent. 1653 len. 19]: This was a most judicious plan of the General 's , as will be proved in the sequel .
Parsing [sent. 1654 len. 52]: Major S -- , with four or five hundred men in three transports , the Larne , and the Mercury , Hon. Company 's brig , were ordered upon this expedition , which sailed at the same time that the army began to march and the boats to ascend the river .
Parsing [sent. 1655 len. 79]: On the arrival at the mouth of the river we found the entrance most formidable in appearance , there being a dozen or more stockades of great extent ; but there were but two manned , the guns of the others , as well as the men , having been forwarded to Donabue , the Burmahs not imagining , as we had so long left that part of their territory unmolested , that we should have attempted it .
Parsing [sent. 1656 len. 44]: Our passage was therefore easy ; after a few broadsides , we landed and spiked the guns , and then , with a fair wind , ran about seventy miles up one of the most picturesque and finest rivers I was ever in .
Parsing [sent. 1657 len. 23]: Occasionally the right lines of stockades presented themselves , but we found nobody in them , and passed by them in peace .
Parsing [sent. 1658 len. 20]: But the river now became more intricate , and the pilots , as usual , knew nothing about it .
Parsing [sent. 1659 len. 30]: It was , however , of little consequence ; the river was deep even at its banks , over which the forest trees threw their boughs in wild luxuriance .
Parsing [sent. 1660 len. 37]: The wind was now down the river , and we were two or three days before we arrived at Bassein , during which we tided and warped how we could , while Major S -- grumbled .
Parsing [sent. 1661 len. 23]: If the reader wishes to know why Major S -- grumbled , I will tell him -- because there was no fighting .
Parsing [sent. 1662 len. 30]: He grumbled when we passed the stockades at the entrance of the river because they were not manned ; and he grumbled at every dismantled stockade that we passed .
Parsing [sent. 1663 len. 56]: But there was no pleasing S -- ; if he was in hard action and not wounded , he grumbled ; if he received a slight wound , he grumbled because it was not a severe one ; if a severe one , he grumbled because he was not able to fight the next day .
Parsing [sent. 1664 len. 17]: He had been nearly cut to pieces in many actions , but he was not content .
Parsing [sent. 1665 len. 41]: Like the man under punishment , the drummer might strike high or strike low , there was no pleasing S -- : nothing but the _ coup de grace _ , if he be now alive , will satisfy him .
Parsing [sent. 1666 len. 16]: But notwithstanding this mania for being carved , he was an excellent and judicious officer .
Parsing [sent. 1667 len. 61]: I have been told he is since dead ; if so , his Majesty has lost one of the most devoted and chivalric officers in his service , to whom might most justly be applied the words of Hotspur , -- `` But if it be a sin to covet honour , I am the most offending soul alive . ''
Parsing [sent. 1668 len. 6]: -LSB- See note 1 . -RSB-
Parsing [sent. 1669 len. 30]: As I before observed , the branches of the trees hung over the sides of the river , and a circumstance occurred which was a source of great amusement .
Parsing [sent. 1670 len. 28]: We had a little monkey , who had been some time on board , and was a favourite , as usual , of the ship 's company .
Parsing [sent. 1671 len. 43]: The baffling winds very often threw us against the banks of the river , near which there was plenty of water ; and when this was the case , the boughs of the trees were interlaced with the rigging of the ship .
Parsing [sent. 1672 len. 61]: This unusual embracing between nature and art gave Jacko the idea of old times when he frolicked in the woods , and unable to resist the force of early associations , he stepped from the top-sail yard to the branch of a large tree , and when the ship had hauled off clear , we found that Jacko had deserted .
Parsing [sent. 1673 len. 23]: We lamented it , and ten minutes afterwards , thinking no more about him , we continued our course up the river .
Parsing [sent. 1674 len. 33]: About an hour had elapsed , during which we had gained upwards of a mile , when again nearing the bank on that side , we heard a loud chattering and screaming .
Parsing [sent. 1675 len. 21]: `` That 's Jacko , sir , '' said one of the men , and others expressed the same opinion .
Parsing [sent. 1676 len. 19]: We manned the jolly-boat , and sent it on shore towards the place where the noise was heard .
Parsing [sent. 1677 len. 54]: The monkey did not wait till the bow of the boat touched the shore , but springing into it when some feet off , he took his seat very deliberately on the stern , and was pulled on board , where immediately he flew up the side , caressing every one he met .
Parsing [sent. 1678 len. 45]: The fact was , that Jacko had found several of his own race in the woods , but , like all wild animals , they immediately attacked one who had worn the chains of servitude , and Jacko had to fly for his life .
Parsing [sent. 1679 len. 32]: We very often interlaced the rigging with the boughs after that , but the monkey remained quiet on the booms , and showed not the slightest wish to renew his rambles .
Parsing [sent. 1680 len. 34]: I think it was on the third day that we arrived below the town of Naputah , which was defended by a very formidable stockade , commanding the whole reach of the river .
Parsing [sent. 1681 len. 45]: The stockade was manned , and we expected that it would be defended , but as we did not fire , neither did they ; and we should have passed it quietly , had not S -- grumbled so much at his bad luck .
Parsing [sent. 1682 len. 18]: The next day we arrived at Bassein , one of the principal towns in the Burman Empire .
Parsing [sent. 1683 len. 44]: Here again the Major was disappointed , for it appeared that , on hearing of the arrival of the expedition at the entrance of the river , the people had divided into two parties , one for resistance , the other for submission .
Parsing [sent. 1684 len. 35]: This difference of opinion had ended in their setting fire to the town and immense magazines of grain , dismantling the stockades , and the major part of the inhabitants flying into the country .
Parsing [sent. 1685 len. 15]: The consequence was , that we took possession of the smoking ruins without opposition .
Parsing [sent. 1686 len. 22]: It was soon observed that the people were tired of the protracted war , and of the desolation occasioned by it .
Parsing [sent. 1687 len. 14]: They wanted to return to their wives and families , who were starving .
Parsing [sent. 1688 len. 59]: But up to this time the chiefs had remained faithful to Bundoola , who had amassed stores and provisions at Bassein , intending to retreat upon it , should he be driven out of the fortress of Donabue ; and as long as he held that fortress , receiving from Bassein his supplies of men and of provisions .
Parsing [sent. 1689 len. 60]: The Burmahs were so unwilling to fight any longer , that they were collected by armed bands , and made prisoners by the chiefs , who sent them up as required ; and many hundreds were still in this way detained , enclosed in stockaded ground , and watched by armed men , in several towns along the river .
Parsing [sent. 1690 len. 51]: An expedition was first despatched up the river , to its junction with the Irrawaddy , as there was a town there in which was the dockyard of the Burmahs , all their war boats , and _ canoes _ of every description being _ built _ at that place .
Parsing [sent. 1691 len. 32]: They ascended without difficulty , and , after a little skirmishing , took possession of the place , burnt all the boats built or building , and then returned to Bassein .
Parsing [sent. 1692 len. 37]: Of course , we had then nothing to do : Major S -- 's orders were to join Sir A Campbell , if he possibly could ; which , with much difficulty , he ultimately effected .
Parsing [sent. 1693 len. 31]: We must now return to the Irrawaddy expedition sent up at the same time that Sir A Campbell marched by land , and our expedition went up the Bassein river .
Parsing [sent. 1694 len. 11]: This force arrived at Donabue before we had gained Bassein .
Parsing [sent. 1695 len. 50]: It found a most formidable fortress , or rather , three fortresses in one , mounting a great number of guns , and , as I before observed , held by Bundoola , the commander of the Burmah forces , in whom the Burmah troops placed the greatest confidence .
Parsing [sent. 1696 len. 34]: I speak from hearsay and memory , but I believe I am correct when I state that there were not less than ten thousand men in Donabue , besides war elephants , etcetera .
Parsing [sent. 1697 len. 46]: Now the river force did not amount in fighting men certainly to one thousand , and they were not in sufficient strength to attack a place of this description , upon which every pains had been taken for a long while to render it impregnable .
Parsing [sent. 1698 len. 44]: The attack was however made , and the smaller stockade of the three carried ; but when they had possession of the smallest stockade , they discovered that they were at the mercy of the second , and in a sort of trap .
Parsing [sent. 1699 len. 19]: The consequence was , defeat -- the only defeat experienced by the white troops during the whole war .
Parsing [sent. 1700 len. 23]: The troops were re-embarked , and the boats were obliged to drop down the river clear of the fire of the fort .
Parsing [sent. 1701 len. 38]: I believe two hundred and fifty English troops were left dead in the stockade , and the next day their bodies , crucified on rafts , were floated down among the English boats by the triumphant Bundoola .
Parsing [sent. 1702 len. 50]: In the meantime a despatch had been sent to Sir A Campbell , who was in advance on the banks of the river ; stating that the force afloat was not able to cope with the fortress , the real strength of which no one had been aware of .
Parsing [sent. 1703 len. 34]: The consequence was , that Sir A Campbell retraced his steps , crossed the river , and attacked it in conjunction with the flotilla , Sir A Campbell taking it in the rear .
Parsing [sent. 1704 len. 31]: After some hard fighting , in which the elephants played their parts , the troops gained possession , and Bundoola having been killed by a shell , the Burmahs fled .
Parsing [sent. 1705 len. 55]: Now it was very fortunate that the expedition had been sent to Bassein , for otherwise the Burmahs would have fallen back upon that place , which held all their stores , and would thus have been able to continue in the rear of Sir A Campbell , as he advanced up the river .
Parsing [sent. 1706 len. 24]: But they had heard of the destruction and capture of Bassein , and consequently directed their flight up the river towards the capital .
Parsing [sent. 1707 len. 73]: We were in possession of all these circumstances shortly after we had taken possession of Bassein ; and although the death of Bundoola and taking of Donabue had dispirited the Burmahs , yet there were many chiefs who still held out , and who , had they crossed with their troops to the Irrawaddy , would have interrupted the supplies coming up , and the wounded and sick who were sent down .
Parsing [sent. 1708 len. 16]: We had , therefore , still the duty of breaking up these resources if possible .
Parsing [sent. 1709 len. 47]: Having ascertained who the parties were , we sent a message to one of the weakest to say , that if he did not tender his submission , and come in to us , we should attack him , and burn the town to the ground .
Parsing [sent. 1710 len. 23]: The chief thought it advisable to obey our summons , and sent word that he would come in on the ensuing day .
Parsing [sent. 1711 len. 44]: He kept his promise : about noon , as we were sitting in the verandah of a large _ Sammy _ house -LRB- a sort of monastery -RRB- , which we had taken possession of , we were informed that he had arrived .
Parsing [sent. 1712 len. 22]: The token of submission on the part of the Burmahs is , presenting the other party with _ wax candles _ .
Parsing [sent. 1713 len. 33]: If a poor man has a request to make , or favour to ask of a great man , he never makes it without laying a small wax candle at his feet .
Parsing [sent. 1714 len. 14]: Neither do they approach the Rayhoon and Mayhoon without this mark of respect .
Parsing [sent. 1715 len. 48]: Some time after this , one of the chiefs who had submitted took up his quarters at Bassein ; and his little daughter , about eight or nine years old , was very fond of coming to see me , as I generally made her little presents .
Parsing [sent. 1716 len. 32]: She became very much attached to me , but she never appeared without a little wax candle , which she dropped at my feet before she threw herself into my lap .
Parsing [sent. 1717 len. 30]: In the present instance , the chief first made his appearance , and having come within a few feet , sat down as a _ mark of respect _ .
Parsing [sent. 1718 len. 29]: He was followed by six more , who each carried about two pounds of wax candles , tastefully arranged in a sort of filigree work of coloured papers .
Parsing [sent. 1719 len. 36]: After these came about fifty men , carrying large baskets full of vegetables and fruit , which they poured out on the floor before us , and then walked away and squatted at a distance .
Parsing [sent. 1720 len. 47]: A few words of ceremony were then exchanged , and the friendship cemented over a bottle of brandy and some wine ; which , notwithstanding the use of spirituous liquors is against their religion , and forbidden by the government , they did not object to .
Parsing [sent. 1721 len. 20]: Before he left I made him a present in return , and he went away delighted with the gift .
Parsing [sent. 1722 len. 91]: Several more of the minor chiefs afterwards came in , and the same formalities were gone through ; but there were three of the most important who would not make their appearance ; one , the chief of Naputah , the town which we had passed , which did not fire at us from the stockades , and two others down at another large arm of the river , who had many men detained for the service of the army if required , and who were still at open defiance .
Parsing [sent. 1723 len. 27]: All these three were gold chatta chiefs , that is , permitted to have a gold umbrella carried over their heads when they appeared in public .
Parsing [sent. 1724 len. 45]: After waiting a certain time for these people to send in their submission , we sent word down to the chief of Naputah , that we should visit him the next day , threatening him with the consequences of not complying with our request .
Parsing [sent. 1725 len. 31]: Accordingly we weighed in the Larne , and dropped down the river till we were abreast of the town and stockade , which was about thirty miles distant from Bassein .
Parsing [sent. 1726 len. 56]: Our broadside was ready ; but as we were about to fire , we perceived that boats were manning , and in about five minutes the chief of Naputah , in his own war-boat , accompanied by about twelve others , and a great many canoes , pulled off from the shore and came alongside .
Parsing [sent. 1727 len. 18]: He made his submission , with the usual accompaniments , and we were soon very good friends .
Parsing [sent. 1728 len. 22]: We gave him a beautiful little brass gun , which ornamented our poop , and he went away very well pleased .
Parsing [sent. 1729 len. 16]: We here had an opportunity of witnessing the dexterity with which they handle their boats .
Parsing [sent. 1730 len. 16]: They really appeared to be alive , they darted through the water with such rapidity .
Parsing [sent. 1731 len. 50]: Many of the Burmahs remained on board , examining every part of the vessel and her equipment ; and soon they were on the best of terms with the seamen and the few troops which I had on board to assist us , for we were very short manned .
Parsing [sent. 1732 len. 49]: We had gained intelligence that there were some guns sunk in a creek , about three miles from Bassein , and we had despatched a boat to look for them , having the assurance of a chief who was at Bassein that the people were peaceable and well-disposed .
Parsing [sent. 1733 len. 30]: By some mistake , the boat went up the wrong creek , and pulled many miles into the country , without finding the spot pointed out by marks given .
Parsing [sent. 1734 len. 21]: At night they were at the mercy of the Burmahs , who came to them to know what they required .
Parsing [sent. 1735 len. 35]: The Burmahs told them that they had mistaken the creek , but were very kind to them , giving them a good supper , and passing the night among them , playing their marionettes .
Parsing [sent. 1736 len. 55]: The next day they showed them their way , and when they came to the guns , the Burmahs dived , and made ropes fast , and brought them up for them , sending a message that they would come and see the _ Great Water-dog _ -LRB- meaning me -RRB- the next day .
Parsing [sent. 1737 len. 47]: We remained two days at anchor , off the town of Naputah , waiting for this boat , as it was our intention to go down the river , and attack the two other gold chatta chiefs , if they did not send in their submission .
Parsing [sent. 1738 len. 46]: On the second day the chief came on board to ask us if we would attend a Nautch which he gave that evening in compliment to us ; but requested that we would not bring all our people , as it would frighten his own .
Parsing [sent. 1739 len. 46]: Although it was not pleasant to trust ourselves on shore in the night , in the midst of so large a force , yet , anxious to make friends with him , we thought it advisable to accept the invitation in the manner he desired .
Parsing [sent. 1740 len. 26]: I replied , `` that I would only bring on shore a few officers , and my usual attendants of six marines without arms . ''
Parsing [sent. 1741 len. 31]: At eight o'clock some of the officers and I went on shore : it was quite dark , but we found the chief at the landing-place ready to receive us .
Parsing [sent. 1742 len. 95]: The marines had their bayonets , and the officers had pistols concealed in case of treachery , and the first lieutenant kept a good look out , with the broadside of the ship all ready at the first flash of a pistol , but these precautions were unnecessary ; the chief took me by the hand and led me up to his house , in front of which had been erected a sort of covered circus , brilliantly lighted up with oil in cocoa-nut shells , and round which were squatted several hundred Burmahs .
Parsing [sent. 1743 len. 44]: He took us all to the raised verandah of the house , which was fitted up for the ceremony , where we found his wife , and all his attendants , but not his daughter , who was said to be very handsome .
Parsing [sent. 1744 len. 12]: As soon as we had taken our seats the Nautch commenced .
Parsing [sent. 1745 len. 23]: About twenty men struck up a very barbarous kind of music , in which the bells and drums made the most noise .
Parsing [sent. 1746 len. 12]: After a few minutes of discordant sound , the play began .
Parsing [sent. 1747 len. 18]: The actors were in a sort of costume , and appeared quite at home in their parts .
Parsing [sent. 1748 len. 55]: The story consisted in the attempts of a young prince to obtain the hand of a young princess ; and the dialogue was constantly interrupted by an actor who appeared to be a looker-on , but who made his remarks upon what passed , so as to excite bursts of laughter from the audience .
Parsing [sent. 1749 len. 22]: He was the Jack Pudding , or wit of the piece , and several of his jokes were not very delicate .
Parsing [sent. 1750 len. 23]: At all events , he was the Liston of the company , for he never spoke nor moved without creating a laugh .
Parsing [sent. 1751 len. 69]: The play ended very curiously ; after the prince had gained the princess , they had a procession , in which they made an imitation of a ship , out of compliment to us ; and then built a little house on the stage with singular rapidity , to the door of which they conducted the youthful couple , closed it , and then the play was over .
Parsing [sent. 1752 len. 66]: In the meantime _ pickled tea _ -LRB- which is a great compliment and excessively nasty -RRB- was handed round to us , and we all partook of it , taking it out with our fingers ; but we could not swallow it , so it remained like a quid of tobacco in our cheeks until we had an opportunity of getting rid of it .
Parsing [sent. 1753 len. 44]: The purser had had the foresight to put a couple of bottles of wine , and one of brandy , in the pockets of the marines , which were now produced , while the band continued to play , and wrestling was introduced .
Parsing [sent. 1754 len. 72]: We asked the chief to join us , but he refused ; he handed down a sort of picture , in which was represented the white elephant , pagodas , etcetera , and told us that he was not only the war chief , but the head of the religion at Naputah , and that it would not be right that he should be seen by his people transgressing the laws .
Parsing [sent. 1755 len. 38]: In the meantime his daughter , who did not come out to us , was very anxious to know what sort of people we were , and she sent for one to be brought in to her .
Parsing [sent. 1756 len. 7]: My clerk was the favoured party .
Parsing [sent. 1757 len. 29]: She examined him very closely , pulled his dress about , made him bare his legs , to see how white they were , and then dismissed him .
Parsing [sent. 1758 len. 36]: The clerk reported her as very handsome , and quite as white as he was ; splendidly dressed ; and with an air of command , which showed that she was aware of her importance .
Parsing [sent. 1759 len. 15]: We staid about two hours longer , and then we rose to go away .
Parsing [sent. 1760 len. 48]: The chief walked with us down to the boats , and we were not sorry to find ourselves on board again ; for the population was much more numerous than we had imagined , and had any treachery been attempted , we must have fallen a sacrifice .
Parsing [sent. 1761 len. 4]: CHAPTER TWENTY SEVEN .
Parsing [sent. 1762 len. 5]: EXPEDITION TO BASSEIN CONTINUED .
Parsing [sent. 1763 len. 4]: May , 1836 .
Parsing [sent. 1764 len. 62]: Although on friendly terms with the chief of Naputah , he was a person of such weight in that part of the country , that it was advisable , if possible , to identify him with us , so that he should never again fall off , and oppose us , in the contingency of a reverse , on the Irrawaddy .
Parsing [sent. 1765 len. 27]: The next day we sent for him , informing him that it was to make him a present in return for his civility the day before .
Parsing [sent. 1766 len. 57]: But before we handed the present to him , we stated our intention of dropping down the river to reduce the two gold chatta chiefs who still held out ; and that , as we did not exactly know where their towns were situated , we wished for some of his people to go with me .
Parsing [sent. 1767 len. 12]: To this first proposition , after some hesitation , he consented .
Parsing [sent. 1768 len. 47]: We then pointed out that our men were not accustomed to work in the sun , and were often ill ; that , as we were now friends , we wished him to allow me some of his boats to assist the ship in the river .
Parsing [sent. 1769 len. 6]: To this also he consented .
Parsing [sent. 1770 len. 77]: In fine , we brought forward our last proposition , which was , that he should supply us with six or eight war-boats , well manned , and that we would pay the men and officers at the same rate per day as we paid our own men ; stating the sum we would give , and that , if he was really sincere in his friendship and goodwill , we expected not to be refused .
Parsing [sent. 1771 len. 44]: Now , among the Burmahs who were with him , there were many whose relations were detained to join the army ; a consultation ensued ; the chief was pressed by his own people , and , at last , gave his consent .
Parsing [sent. 1772 len. 40]: We then presented him with the piece of plate , upon which his eyes had constantly been turned , and he went away , promising us that the men and boats should be alongside by daylight the next morning .
Parsing [sent. 1773 len. 36]: This chief adhered to his promise , and we weighed anchor the next day , and made sail down with the war-boats , and three or four despatch-canoes , pulled by four or five men .
Parsing [sent. 1774 len. 48]: These little canoes , when put to their speed , dashed through the water at such a rate , that they threw off from each bow one continued little fan-shaped _ jet-d'eau _ , which had a very beautiful appearance , the sunbeams forming them into rainbows .
Parsing [sent. 1775 len. 59]: As for our Burmah force , they were at one time pulling against the vessel sailing ; at others , hanging on , and the people climbing about the rigging , and ascending the masthead of the vessel ; but they soon all congregated to the stand of muskets , for that was the great object of attraction .
Parsing [sent. 1776 len. 26]: In the afternoon we had ball practice with the small arms ; and the Burmahs were , much to their delight , permitted to fire .
Parsing [sent. 1777 len. 20]: It is surprising how exact they were in their aims , considering the little practice they must have had .
Parsing [sent. 1778 len. 45]: Bad as all the muskets are which are served out to the ships of war , I really believe that there was not a Burmah who would not have laid down every thing he possessed , except his life , to have obtained one .
Parsing [sent. 1779 len. 34]: One of them , when he was permitted to take a musket , looked proudly round , and said , with a smile of joy , `` Now I 'm a man ! ''
Parsing [sent. 1780 len. 19]: The next day we arrived at the branch of the river where one of the chiefs held out .
Parsing [sent. 1781 len. 74]: At daylight our own boats were manned , and with the Burmah boats ranged in line , made an imposing appearance , which was very necessary , for at that time we were so short-handed , that we could not send away more than forty men -- a force so small , that , had the Burmahs opposed to us seen it advance , they would probably have tried their strength with us .
Parsing [sent. 1782 len. 52]: As it was , we pulled into the stockaded town in a line , the despatch-boats flying across us backwards and forwards like porpoises before the bows of a ship running down the trades : not that they had any messages to carry , but merely to show their own dexterity .
Parsing [sent. 1783 len. 55]: When we had advanced to within a quarter of a mile , a boat came out and communicated with one of the despatch-boats , saying that the Burmahs would not fight if we did not attack them , and that they would deliver up the men detained , and their chief as a prisoner .
Parsing [sent. 1784 len. 30]: We agreed to these terms , landed , took possession of the chief with his gold chatta , correspondence with Bundoolah , etcetera , and took him on board .
Parsing [sent. 1785 len. 28]: On this occasion , we would not trust the Burmahs employed with muskets ; it was too soon ; they had only their own swords and spears .
Parsing [sent. 1786 len. 12]: The chief was a fine tall man with a long beard .
Parsing [sent. 1787 len. 35]: Like all Burmahs , he took his loss of liberty very composedly , sitting down between the guns with his attendants , and only expressing his indignation at the treachery of his own people .
Parsing [sent. 1788 len. 45]: We were very anxious to know what had become of the guns of the dismantled stockade , which were said to be in his possession , but he positively denied it , saying that they had been despatched in boats across to the Irrawaddy .
Parsing [sent. 1789 len. 61]: Whether this were true or not , it was impossible to say ; but , at all events , it was necessary to make some further attempts to obtain them , so we told him , that if he did not inform us where the guns were , by the next morning his head would be taken off his shoulders .
Parsing [sent. 1790 len. 13]: At this pleasant intelligence he opened his betel-bag and renewed his quid .
Parsing [sent. 1791 len. 75]: The next day he was summoned forth to account for the said guns , and again protested that they had been sent to Donabue , which I really believe was false , as they were not taken out of the stockade until after Donabue was in the possession of Sir A. Campbell : it was therefore judged proper to appear to proceed to extremities ; and this time it was done with more form .
Parsing [sent. 1792 len. 20]: A file of marines was marched aft with their muskets , and the sergeant appeared with his drawn sword .
Parsing [sent. 1793 len. 40]: Sand was strewed on the deck in front of the marines ; and he was led there and ordered to kneel down , so that his head , if cut off , would fall where the sand was strewn .
Parsing [sent. 1794 len. 33]: He was again asked if he would tell where the guns were concealed , and again stated that they were at Donabue ; upon which he was desired to prepare for death .
Parsing [sent. 1795 len. 33]: He called one of his attendants and gave him his silver betel-box , saying , `` Take this to my wife , -- when she sees it she will know all . ''
Parsing [sent. 1796 len. 30]: I watched him very closely ; his countenance was composed , but , as he bent forward over the sand , the muscles of his arms and shoulders quivered .
Parsing [sent. 1797 len. 39]: However , as it is not the custom to cut off people 's heads on the quarter-deck of his Majesty 's ships , we very magnanimously reprieved him , and he was afterwards sent a prisoner to Calcutta .
Parsing [sent. 1798 len. 17]: But that he had the guns , we discovered afterwards , which adds to his merit .
Parsing [sent. 1799 len. 23]: Having succeeded in this attempt , we made sail for the stockade of the other chief , and arrived there that evening .
Parsing [sent. 1800 len. 48]: As he was supposed to be greater in force than the other , we decided upon an attack in the dark , when he would not be able to distinguish of what our force was composed ; and this time we gave muskets to our Burmah comrades .
Parsing [sent. 1801 len. 32]: The attack was successful , we obtained possession , and the chief fled , but our Burmahs pursued him nearly two miles , made him prisoner , and brought him aboard .
Parsing [sent. 1802 len. 21]: As he immediately tendered his submission , which the other would not do , he was released the next day .
Parsing [sent. 1803 len. 39]: We had done all our work , and having employed the Burmahs for a few days more in destroying the stockades at the entrance of the river , they were paid and discharged from his Majesty 's service .
Parsing [sent. 1804 len. 45]: They would not , however , quit us ; but , so long as we remained in the river they continued to hang on to the ship , and discovered three guns which had been sunk , which they weighed and brought on board .
Parsing [sent. 1805 len. 21]: I have entered into this short narrative , as it will give some idea of the character of these people .
Parsing [sent. 1806 len. 70]: The government is despotic , cruel , and treacherous , but the people are neither cruel nor treacherous : on the contrary , I think they would make most excellent and faithful soldiers ; and it is singular to find , surrounded by natives who have not the slightest energy of mind or body , a people so active , so laborious , and so enterprising as the Burmahs .
Parsing [sent. 1807 len. 27]: The English seamen are particularly partial to them , and declared they were `` the best set of chaps they had ever fallen in with . ''
Parsing [sent. 1808 len. 13]: They admitted the Burmahs to their messes , and were sworn friends .
Parsing [sent. 1809 len. 35]: I forgot to say , that when the chiefs sent in their submissions , at first , among other presents , they sent _ slaves _ , usually females , which was rather awkward .
Parsing [sent. 1810 len. 30]: But not wishing to affront them , I begged that the slaves sent might be children , and not grown up , as we had no accommodation for them .
Parsing [sent. 1811 len. 29]: The consequence was , that I had quite a young family when I left the river , which I distributed at Rangoon and the presidencies on my return .
Parsing [sent. 1812 len. 28]: For if they were only bond-slaves , which I suppose they were , it was a kindness to have them educated and taken care _ of _ .
Parsing [sent. 1813 len. 27]: We lost one little fellow , that was a great favourite with the men ; he was about three years old , and could speak English .
Parsing [sent. 1814 len. 19]: He had been christened by the sailors Billy Bamboo , and was quite as amusing as the monkey .
Parsing [sent. 1815 len. 17]: The poor little fellow died very suddenly , and was much regretted by all on board .
Parsing [sent. 1816 len. 59]: I certainly do think that we may eventually find the Burmahs to be the most powerful enemy that we shall have to contend with in India ; and , at the same time , I can not help giving my opinion as to the ridiculous fear we entertain of the Russians ever interfering with us in that quarter .
Parsing [sent. 1817 len. 64]: That the extension of the Russian empire has been a favourite object through many of her dynasties , is true : but it is so no longer : they have discovered that already their empire is too extensive ; and hardly a year passes but they have outbreaks and insurrections to quell in quarters so remote that they are scarcely heard of here .
Parsing [sent. 1818 len. 51]: That Russia might _ possibly _ lead an army through our Indian possessions , I admit ; but that she never could hold them if she did do so , is equally certain ; the conquest would be useless to her , after having been obtained at an enormous sacrifice .
Parsing [sent. 1819 len. 113]: The fact is , the Russians -LRB- with the exception of the Emperor Paul -RRB- never had any intention of the kind , and _ never will attempt it _ : but they have discovered how very alive we are to the possibility , and how very jealous and anxious we are on the subject , and it is possible that they have made demonstrations in that direction to alarm us ; but I think myself , that the great object of Russia in these advances has been to force a channel for trade , which in her present situation she is to the south of her extensive empire nearly deprived of .
Parsing [sent. 1820 len. 68]: Notwithstanding the outcry which has so often been raised against the Russian empire , it has always appeared to me that _ our natural ally _ is Russia ; as for an alliance with France it is morally impossible that two rival nations like us can continue very long at peace ; our interests are separate and conflicting , and our jealousy but sleeps for the moment .
Parsing [sent. 1821 len. 62]: We have been at peace with France many years , and have not yet succeeded in making a satisfactory commercial treaty with her ; neither will any of the other Continental powers permit our manufactures to enter , with the exception of Russia , who not only takes them , but returns to us what is most valuable for our marine .
Parsing [sent. 1822 len. 12]: Why , then , this outcry against the ambition of Russia ?
Parsing [sent. 1823 len. 9]: nothing but tirades against _ Russian _ ambition .
Parsing [sent. 1824 len. 6]: Does France show no ambition ?
Parsing [sent. 1825 len. 6]: Does America show no ambition ?
Parsing [sent. 1826 len. 6]: Have we no ambition ourselves ?
Parsing [sent. 1827 len. 33]: Why this constant suspicion and doubt against a power whose interest it is to be closely allied to us , and who can always prove a valuable aid in case of emergency ?
Parsing [sent. 1828 len. 14]: -- simply because Russia wishes to have an opening to the Black Sea .
Parsing [sent. 1829 len. 28]: And this is very natural ; her northern ports are closed nine months in the year , and therefore her navy and mercantile marine are almost useless .
Parsing [sent. 1830 len. 11]: She has no outlet , no means of raising either .
Parsing [sent. 1831 len. 9]: Does she , then , ask too much ?
Parsing [sent. 1832 len. 24]: Is a great empire like Russia to be blocked up , her commerce and navy crippled , for the want of an outlet ?
Parsing [sent. 1833 len. 17]: She does require the opening of the Black Sea ; it is all that she requires .
Parsing [sent. 1834 len. 32]: She never will remain quiet until she obtains it ; and obtain it sooner or later she certainly will ; and in my opinion she is perfectly justified in her attempts .
Parsing [sent. 1835 len. 9]: What would be the consequence if she succeeded ?
Parsing [sent. 1836 len. 50]: -- that , if we were wise enough to continue on terms of amity with Russia , who has invariably extended the hand of friendship to us , and has I believe never failed in her treaties , we should have a balance of power to us very important .
Parsing [sent. 1837 len. 11]: Whose navies shall we in future have to contend against ?
Parsing [sent. 1838 len. 32]: -- those of France and America ; for it is certain that whenever we go to war with France , America will back her , and their navies will be united .
Parsing [sent. 1839 len. 49]: At present , the navy of America is not very large , but it can soon be made so ; and we should not be sorry to have the navy of Russia on our side , to balance against the two which will always be opposed to us .
Parsing [sent. 1840 len. 30]: It is , therefore , our interest to _ assist _ Russia in the object she has in view , and to keep up a firm alliance with her .
Parsing [sent. 1841 len. 68]: It is the interest of France to excite jealousies between Russia and this country ; and her emissaries have been but too successful , at the very time that France has , contrary to all treaty , and exclaiming against _ Russian _ ambition , seized upon Algiers , and is now playing her game , so as if possible to command the whole of the Mediterranean .
Parsing [sent. 1842 len. 40]: The very strides which France has made in that quarter should point out to us the propriety of opening the Black Sea for Russia , so as to restore the balance of power in that future site of contention .
Parsing [sent. 1843 len. 122]: I repeat that we are blind in every way to our own interests , in not uniting ourselves firmly by an alliance , offensive and defensive , with Russia ; and that by so doing , we should be the greatest gainers ; for with France we must never expect more than a _ hollow truce _ , concealing for the time her jealousy and thirst for revenge , -- a truce during which her secret efforts to undermine us , will be still carried on as indefatigably as ever , and which must only be considered as a mere feint to recover her breath , before she again renews her frenzied efforts to humiliate England , and obtain universal dominion .
Parsing [sent. 1844 len. 4]: CHAPTER TWENTY EIGHT .
Parsing [sent. 1845 len. 6]: London , June , 1837 .
Parsing [sent. 1846 len. 29]: To one who has visited foreign climes , how very substantial everything appears in England , from the child 's plaything to the Duke of York 's column !
Parsing [sent. 1847 len. 12]: To use a joiners phrase , everything abroad is comparatively scamp-work .
Parsing [sent. 1848 len. 56]: Talk about the Palais Royale , the Rue Richelieu , and the splendour of the Parisian shops -- why , two hundred yards of Regent-street , commencing from Howell and James 's , would buy the whole of them , and leave a balance sufficient to buy the remainder of the French _ expositions _ .
Parsing [sent. 1849 len. 18]: But still , if more substantial and massive , we are at the same time also heavy .
Parsing [sent. 1850 len. 43]: We want more space , more air , more room to breathe , in London ; we are too closely packed ; we want gardens with trees to absorb the mephitic air , for what our lungs reject is suitable to vegetation .
Parsing [sent. 1851 len. 19]: But we can not have all we want in this world , so we must do without them .
Parsing [sent. 1852 len. 9]: What wealth is now pouring into the country !
Parsing [sent. 1853 len. 34]: and , thank God , it is now somewhat better expended than it was in the bubble mania , which acted upon the plethora certainly , but bled us too freely and uselessly .
Parsing [sent. 1854 len. 66]: The rail-road speculators have taken off many millions , and the money is well employed ; for even allowing that , in some instances , the expectations of the parties who speculate may be disappointed , still it is spent in the country ; and not only is it affording employment and sustenance to thousands , but the staple produce of England only is consumed .
Parsing [sent. 1855 len. 20]: In these speculations -- in the millions required and immediately produced , you can witness the superiority of England .
Parsing [sent. 1856 len. 20]: Undertakings from which foreign governments would shrink with dismay are here effected by the meeting of a few individuals .
Parsing [sent. 1857 len. 6]: And now for my commissions .
Parsing [sent. 1858 len. 4]: What a list !
Parsing [sent. 1859 len. 64]: And the first item is -- two Canary birds , the last having been one fine morning found dead : nobody knows how ; there was plenty of seed and water -LRB- put in after the servant found that they had been starved by his neglect -RRB- , which , of course , proved that they did not die for want of food .
Parsing [sent. 1860 len. 26]: I hate what are called pets ; they are a great nuisance , for they will die , and then such a lamentation over them !
Parsing [sent. 1861 len. 48]: In the `` Fire Worshippers '' Moore makes his Hinda say -- `` I never nursed a dear gazelle , To glad me with its soft black eye , But when it came to know me well And love me -- it was sure to die . ''
Parsing [sent. 1862 len. 15]: Now Hinda was perfectly correct , except in thinking that she was peculiarly unfortunate .
Parsing [sent. 1863 len. 13]: Every one who keeps pets might tell the same tale as Hinda .
Parsing [sent. 1864 len. 73]: I recollect once a Canary bird died , and my young people were in a great tribulation ; so to amuse them we made them a paper coffin , put the defunct therein , and sewed on the lid , dug a grave in the garden , and dressing them out in any remnants of black we could find for weepers , made a procession to the grave where it was buried .
Parsing [sent. 1865 len. 8]: This little divertissement quite took their fancy .
Parsing [sent. 1866 len. 25]: The next day one of the youngest came up to me and said , `` Oh , papa , when will you die ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1867 len. 17]: -- A strange question , thought I , quite forgetting the procession of the day before .
Parsing [sent. 1868 len. 11]: -- `` Why do you ask , my dear ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1869 len. 14]: -- `` Oh , because it will be such fun burying you . ''
Parsing [sent. 1870 len. 11]: -- `` Much obliged to you , my love . ''
Parsing [sent. 1871 len. 11]: There is much more intellect in birds than people suppose .
Parsing [sent. 1872 len. 25]: An instance of that occurred the other day , at a slate quarry belonging to a friend , from whom I have the narrative .
Parsing [sent. 1873 len. 38]: A thrush , not aware of the expansive properties of gunpowder , thought proper to build her nest on a ridge of the quarry , in the very centre of which they were constantly blasting the rock .
Parsing [sent. 1874 len. 56]: At first she was very much discomposed by the fragments flying in all directions , but still she would not , quit her chosen locality ; she soon observed that a bell rang whenever a train was about to be fired , and that , at the notice , the workmen retired to safe positions .
Parsing [sent. 1875 len. 33]: In a few days , when she heard the bell , she quitted her exposed situation , and flew down to where the workmen sheltered themselves , dropping close to their feet .
Parsing [sent. 1876 len. 18]: There she would remain until the explosion had taken place , and then return to her nest .
Parsing [sent. 1877 len. 26]: The workmen , observing this , narrated it to their employers , and it was also told to visitors who came to view the quarry .
Parsing [sent. 1878 len. 50]: The visitors naturally expressed a wish to witness so curious a specimen of intellect ; but , as the rock could not always be ready to be blasted when visitors came , the bell was rung instead , and , for a few times , answered the same purpose .
Parsing [sent. 1879 len. 95]: The thrush flew down close to where they stood ; but she perceived that she was trifled with , and it interfered with her process of incubation : the consequence was , that afterwards , when the bell was rung , she would peep over the ledge to ascertain if the workmen did retreat , and if they did not , she would remain where she was , probably saying to herself , `` No , no , gentlemen ; I 'm not to be roused off my eggs merely for your amusement . ''
Parsing [sent. 1880 len. 15]: Some birds have a great deal of humour in them , particularly the raven .
Parsing [sent. 1881 len. 17]: One that belonged to me was the most mischievous and amusing creature I ever met with .
Parsing [sent. 1882 len. 56]: He would get into the flower-garden , go to the beds where the gardener had sowed a great variety of seeds , with sticks put in the ground with labels , and then he would amuse himself with pulling up every stick , and laying them in heaps of ten or twelve on the path .
Parsing [sent. 1883 len. 16]: This used to irritate the old gardener very much , who would drive him away .
Parsing [sent. 1884 len. 19]: The raven knew that he ought not to do it , or he would not have done it .
Parsing [sent. 1885 len. 69]: He would soon return to his mischief , and when the gardener again chased him -LRB- the old man could not walk very fast -RRB- , the raven would keep just clear of the rake or hoc in his hand , dancing back before him , and singing as plain as a man could , `` Tol de rol de rol ; tol de rol de rol ! ''
Parsing [sent. 1886 len. 7]: with all kinds of mimicking gestures .
Parsing [sent. 1887 len. 19]: The bird is alive now , and continues the same meritorious practice whenever he can find an opportunity .
Parsing [sent. 1888 len. 4]: CHAPTER TWENTY NINE .
Parsing [sent. 1889 len. 6]: June , Steam-boat Princess Victoria .
Parsing [sent. 1890 len. 35]: It certainly appears that the motion of a steam-vessel produces more nausea than that of a sailing-vessel ; and people appear to suffer in some degree in proportion to the power of the engines .
Parsing [sent. 1891 len. 17]: This may be accounted for by the vibration of the vessel increasing in the same ratio .
Parsing [sent. 1892 len. 31]: We are now in a vessel of two hundred and fifty horse power , and the consequence is that the passengers are as sick as two hundred and fifty horses .
Parsing [sent. 1893 len. 17]: The effect of the vibration of the after part of the vessel amounts to the ridiculous .
Parsing [sent. 1894 len. 33]: When dinner was put on the table , we had no occasion for a bell to announce it , for every glass on the table was dancing to its own jingling music .
Parsing [sent. 1895 len. 28]: And when the covers were taken off , it was still more absurd -- everything in the dishes appeared to be infected with Saint Vitus 's dance .
Parsing [sent. 1896 len. 90]: The boiled leg of mutton shook its collops of fat at a couple of fowls which figured in a sarabande round and round their own dish , -- roast beef shifted about with a slow and stately movement -- a ham _ glisseed croisee _ from one side to the other -- tongues wagged that were never meant to wag again -- bottles reeled and fell over like drunken men , and your piece of bread constantly ran away and was to be pulled back into its proper place .
Parsing [sent. 1897 len. 20]: It was a regular jig-a-jig -- a country-dance of pousette , down the middle , and right and left .
Parsing [sent. 1898 len. 48]: The communication of motion was strange ; the whole company seated on long forms were jig-a-jigging up and down together -- your knife jigged and your fork jigged -- even the morsel which was put into your mouth gave one more jump before it could be seized .
Parsing [sent. 1899 len. 25]: However , we jigged it to some purpose ; for , in eighteen hours and a half , we passed from London to Antwerp .
Parsing [sent. 1900 len. 18]: The English are naturally great _ voyageurs _ : the feeling is inherent from our insular position .
Parsing [sent. 1901 len. 73]: I have been reflecting whether I can recollect , in my whole life , ever to have been three months in one place , but I can not , nor do I believe that I ever was -- not even when sent to school ; for I used to run away every quarter , just to see how my family were -- an amiable weakness , which even flogging could not eradicate .
Parsing [sent. 1902 len. 94]: And then I was off to sea ; there I had my wish , as Shakespeare says , borne away by `` the viewless winds , and blown with restless violence about the pendent world , '' north , south , east , and west ; one month freezing , the next burning ; all nations , all colours , -- white , copper , brown , and black ; all scenery , from the blasted pine towering amidst the frost and snow , to the cocoa-nut waving its leaves to the sea-breeze .
Parsing [sent. 1903 len. 28]: Well , `` homekeeping youths have homely wits , '' says the same author ; and he has told more truth than any man who ever wrote .
Parsing [sent. 1904 len. 35]: I certainly did hear of one young man who did not gain much by travelling ; he was a banker 's clerk , and obtained three months ' vacation to go on the Continent .
Parsing [sent. 1905 len. 25]: He landed at Ostend , and the next day found himself in the track-schuyt that is towed by horses , from Bruges to Ghent .
Parsing [sent. 1906 len. 32]: The cabins were magnificent , velvet and gold the down cushions luxurious , the dinner and breakfast sumptuous , the wine excellent , the bed-rooms comfortable , and the expense moderate .
Parsing [sent. 1907 len. 7]: Moreover , the motion was imperceptible .
Parsing [sent. 1908 len. 7]: What could a man wish more ?
Parsing [sent. 1909 len. 64]: He arrived at Ghent , and could not make his mind up to quit this barge ; so he returned in her to Bruges , and then back again to Ghent ; and thus he continued between the two towns , backwards and forwards , until the three months ' leave had expired , and he was obliged to return to the desk .
Parsing [sent. 1910 len. 19]: I have never yet made up my mind whether this personage was a wise man or a fool .
Parsing [sent. 1911 len. 77]: But , until the opening of the Continent , the English were only voyageurs , not travellers ; and that , after having been so long debarred , they should be desirous of visiting the various portions of Europe , is not only natural but praiseworthy ; but that they should make the Continent their residence -- should expatriate themselves altogether , is , to me , a source of astonishment as well as of regret .
Parsing [sent. 1912 len. 7]: The excuse offered is the cheapness .
Parsing [sent. 1913 len. 95]: It is but an excuse , for I deny it to be the fact : I have visited most places , with and without a family ; and I will positively assert , not for the benefit of others who have already expatriated themselves , but as a check to those who feel so inclined , that they will discover too soon that , at less expense , they can command more good living and substantial comforts in England , than in any part of the Continent they may fix upon as their habitation .
Parsing [sent. 1914 len. 9]: Let us enter a little into the subject .
Parsing [sent. 1915 len. 13]: First , as to the capitals , Paris , Brussels , etcetera .
Parsing [sent. 1916 len. 28]: Let it first be remembered that we have no longer war prices in England , that almost every article has fallen from thirty-five to fifty per cent .
Parsing [sent. 1917 len. 37]: It is true that some tradespeople who are established as fashionable keep up their prices ; but it is not absolutely necessary to employ them , as there are those equally skilled who are more moderate .
Parsing [sent. 1918 len. 36]: But even the most fashionable have been obliged , to a certain degree , to lower their prices ; and their present prices , reduced as they are , will most assuredly die with them .
Parsing [sent. 1919 len. 21]: Everything will , by degrees , find its level ; but this level is not to be found at once .
Parsing [sent. 1920 len. 46]: Should peace continue , ten years from this date will make a great alteration in every article , not only of necessity , but of luxury ; and then , after having been the dearest , England will become the cheapest residence in the world .
Parsing [sent. 1921 len. 20]: House rent in the capitals abroad is certainly as dear , if not dearer than it is in England .
Parsing [sent. 1922 len. 25]: There are situations more or less fashionable in every metropolis ; and if you wish to reside in those quarters , you pay accordingly .
Parsing [sent. 1923 len. 70]: It is true that , by taking a portion of a house , you to a certain degree indemnify yourself ; -- a first , second , or third story , with a common staircase loaded with dirt and filth ; but is this equal to the comfort of a clean English house , in which you have your own servants , and are not overlooked by your neighbours ?
Parsing [sent. 1924 len. 26]: If they were to let out houses in floors in England as they do in Paris and elsewhere , a less sum would be demanded .
Parsing [sent. 1925 len. 23]: You may procure a handsome house in a fashionable quarter , well furnished , in London , for 300 pounds per annum .
Parsing [sent. 1926 len. 73]: Go to the Place Vendome , or those quarters styled the English quarters , at Paris , and which are by no means the most fashionable quarters , and you will pay for a handsome front floor 700 francs per month ; so that for one floor of a house in Paris you will pay 336 pounds per annum , when in London you will obtain the whole house for 300 pounds .
Parsing [sent. 1927 len. 22]: The proprietor of the Paris house , therefore , receives much more by letting his floors separate than the English do .
Parsing [sent. 1928 len. 30]: The common articles of necessity are as dear , if not dearer abroad ; the _ octroi _ duty upon all that enters the barriers raising the price excessively .
Parsing [sent. 1929 len. 53]: Meat at Paris or Brussels is as dear as in London , and not so good ; it is as dear , because they charge you the same price all round , about 5 pence per pound , independent of its inferiority and the villainous manner in which it is cut up .
Parsing [sent. 1930 len. 14]: Our butchers only butcher the animal , but foreign butchers butcher the meat .
Parsing [sent. 1931 len. 14]: Poultry is as dear ; game much dearer ; and so is fish .
Parsing [sent. 1932 len. 13]: Indeed , fish is not only dear , but scarce and bad .
Parsing [sent. 1933 len. 17]: Horses and carriages are quite as dear abroad , in the capitals , as in London .
Parsing [sent. 1934 len. 26]: Clothes are in some respects cheaper , in others dearer , especially articles of English manufacture , which are more sought after than any others .
Parsing [sent. 1935 len. 34]: Amusements are said to be cheaper ; but , admitting that , the places of amusement are oftener resorted to , and in consequence as much money is spent abroad as in England .
Parsing [sent. 1936 len. 57]: It is true that there are an immense number of theatres in Paris , and that most of them are very reasonable in their charges for admission ; but be it recollected that there are not above three of them which are considered fashionable , if even respectable ; and there the prices are sufficiently high .
Parsing [sent. 1937 len. 53]: If people went to Sadler 's Wells , the Coburg , Victoria , Queen 's Theatre , Astley 's , and other minor theatres in London , as they do to the Theatres Saint Martin , Gymnase , et Varietes at Paris , they would find no great difference in the prices .
Parsing [sent. 1938 len. 6]: What then is there cheaper ?
Parsing [sent. 1939 len. 2]: Wine .
Parsing [sent. 1940 len. 16]: I grant it ; and , it is also asserted , the education of children .
Parsing [sent. 1941 len. 35]: We will pass over these two last points for the present , and examine whether living is cheaper on the Continent , provided you do _ not _ hive in any of the capitals .
Parsing [sent. 1942 len. 56]: That at Tours and other places in the south of France , at Genoa , at Bruges , in Belgium , you may live cheaper than in London , I grant ; but if any one means to assert that you can live cheaper than in the country in England , I deny it altogether .
Parsing [sent. 1943 len. 16]: People go abroad , and select the cheapest parts of the Continent to live in .
Parsing [sent. 1944 len. 45]: If they were to do the same in England , they would find that they could live much cheaper and much better ; for instance , in Devonshire , Cornwall , and Wales , and , indeed , in almost every county in England .
Parsing [sent. 1945 len. 21]: The fact is , it is not the cheapness of the living which induces so many people to reside abroad .
Parsing [sent. 1946 len. 22]: There are many reasons ; and as I wish to be charitable , I will put forward the most favourable ones .
Parsing [sent. 1947 len. 25]: In England , we are money-making people , and we have the aristocracy of wealth as well _ as _ the aristocracy of rank .
Parsing [sent. 1948 len. 28]: It has long been the custom for many people to live beyond their incomes , and to keep up an appearance which their means have not warranted .
Parsing [sent. 1949 len. 21]: Many , especially the landed proprietors , finding their rentals reduced from various causes , have been necessitated to retrench .

Parsing [sent. 1952 len. 26]: Should those return who have emigrated for the above reasons , they would find that this striving for show is hardly perceptible now in England .
Parsing [sent. 1953 len. 22]: Those who have remained have either had sense enough , or have been forced by circumstances , to reduce their expenditure .

Parsing [sent. 1955 len. 43]: Certainly there are a sufficient number of Counts , Viscounts , and Marquesses to associate with ; but in France high birth is not proved by titles , which are of little or no value , and do not even establish gentility .
Parsing [sent. 1956 len. 39]: This society may certainly be entered into at a much less expense than that of England , especially in the metropolis ; but , depend upon it , there is a species of society dear at any price .
Parsing [sent. 1957 len. 39]: With respect to education of children , that boys may receive advantage from a Continental education I admit ; but woe be to the mother who intrusts her daughter to the ruin of a French _ Pension _ !
Parsing [sent. 1958 len. 45]: In England there are many excellent schools in the country , as cheap and cheaper than on the Continent : but the schoolmasters near London , generally speaking , are ruining them by their adherence to the old system , and their extravagant terms .
Parsing [sent. 1959 len. 63]: The _ system _ of education on the Continent is certainly superior to that of England , and the attention to the pupils is greater : of course there are bad schools abroad as well as in England ; but the balance is much in favour of those on the Continent , with the advantage of being at nearly one-half the expense .
Parsing [sent. 1960 len. 35]: A great alteration has taken place in modern education ; the living languages and mathematics have been found to be preferable to the classics and other instruction still adhered to in the English schools .
Parsing [sent. 1961 len. 25]: I have always considered , and have every reason to be confirmed in my opinion , that the foundation of all education is mathematics .
Parsing [sent. 1962 len. 34]: Every thing else may be obtained by rote , and without thinking ; but from the elements of arithmetic up to Euclid and algebra , no boy can work his task without thinking .
Parsing [sent. 1963 len. 36]: I never yet knew a man who was a good mathematician who was not well-informed upon almost every point ; and the reason is clear -- mathematics have prepared his mind to receive and retain .
Parsing [sent. 1964 len. 35]: In all foreign schools this important branch of education is more attended to than it is in England ; and that alone would be a sufficient reason for me to give them the preference .
Parsing [sent. 1965 len. 44]: In point of morals , I consider the schools of both countries much upon a par ; although , from the system abroad of never debasing a child by corporal punishment , I give the foreign schools the preference even in that point .
Parsing [sent. 1966 len. 35]: I consider , then , that boys are better educated abroad than in England , and acquire much more correctly the living languages , which are of more use to them than the classics .
Parsing [sent. 1967 len. 25]: So much I can say in favour of the Continent ; but in every other respect I consider the advantage in favour of England .

Parsing [sent. 2082 len. 104]: But it too often happens that the public will form their opinion of an author from opinions put by him into the mouths of the characters drawn in a work of fiction , forgetting that in these instances it is not the author who speaks , but the individuals which his imagination has conjured up ; and that the opinions expressed by these creatures of his brain , although perfectly in keeping with the character , and necessary to produce that _ vraisemblance _ which is the great merit of fiction , may be entirely opposed to the real sentiments of the author .

Parsing [sent. 2180 len. 11]: How changed are our feelings as we advance in life !

Parsing [sent. 2182 len. 14]: In youth we live but for ourselves -- self predominates in every thing .
Parsing [sent. 2183 len. 24]: In mature age , if we have fulfilled the conditions of our tenure , we feel that we must live for our children .

Parsing [sent. 2742 len. 18]: The consequence of this difficulty was , that the rats were not punished for contempt of court .

Parsing [sent. 2744 len. 13]: It is a point on which we are very unenlightened in England .
